<p>The reasons why women have abortions are diverse and vary across the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guttmacher_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-guttmacher-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bankole98_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-bankole98-8">[7]</a></sup> Some of the reasons may include an inability to afford a child, domestic violence, lack of support, feeling they are too young, and the wish to complete education or advance a career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-9">[8]</a></sup> Additional reasons include not being able or willing to raise a child conceived as a result of rape or incest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bankole98_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-bankole98-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finer2005_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-finer2005-167">[166]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Societal">Societal</span></h3>
<p>Some abortions are undergone as the result of societal pressures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup> These might include the preference for children of a specific sex or race, disapproval of single or early motherhood, stigmatization of people with disabilities, insufficient economic support for families, lack of access to or rejection of contraceptive methods, or efforts toward <a href="/wiki/Population_control" title="Population control">population control</a> (such as China's <a href="/wiki/One-child_policy" title="One-child policy">one-child policy</a>). These factors can sometimes result in compulsory abortion or <a href="/wiki/Sex-selective_abortion" title="Sex-selective abortion">sex-selective abortion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MissingWomen_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-MissingWomen-169">[168]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Maternal_and_fetal_health">Maternal and fetal health</span></h3>
<p>An additional factor is maternal health which was listed as the main reason by about a third of women in 3 of 27 countries and about 7% of women in a further 7 of these 27 countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guttmacher_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-guttmacher-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bankole98_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-bankole98-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the U.S., the Supreme Court decisions in <i><a href="/wiki/Roe_v._Wade" title="Roe v. Wade">Roe v. Wade</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Doe_v._Bolton" title="Doe v. Bolton">Doe v. Bolton</a></i>: "ruled that the state's interest in the life of the fetus became compelling only at the point of viability, defined as the point at which the fetus can survive independently of its mother. Even after the point of viability, the state cannot favor the life of the fetus over the life or health of the pregnant woman. Under the right of privacy, physicians must be free to use their "medical judgment for the preservation of the life or health of the mother." On the same day that the Court decided Roe, it also decided Doe v. Bolton, in which the Court defined health very broadly: "The medical judgment may be exercised in the light of all factors—physical, emotional, psychological, familial, and the woman's age—relevant to the well-being of the patient. All these factors may relate to health. This allows the attending physician the room he needs to make his best medical judgment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1200–01">: 1200–01 </span></sup>
</p><p>Public opinion shifted in America following television personality <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sherri_Finkbine" title="Sherri Finkbine">Sherri Finkbine</a>'s discovery during her fifth month of pregnancy that she had been exposed to <a href="/wiki/Thalidomide" title="Thalidomide">thalidomide</a>. Unable to obtain a legal abortion in the United States, she traveled to Sweden. From 1962 to 1965, an outbreak of <a href="/wiki/Rubella" title="Rubella">German measles</a> left 15,000 babies with severe birth defects. In 1967, the <a href="/wiki/American_Medical_Association" title="American Medical Association">American Medical Association</a> publicly supported liberalization of abortion laws. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Opinion_Research_Center" title="National Opinion Research Center">National Opinion Research Center</a> poll in 1965 showed 73% supported abortion when the mother's life was at risk, 57% when birth defects were present and 59% for pregnancies resulting from rape or incest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoan200757_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoan200757-171">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Update plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Update" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="Ambox current red Asia Australia.svg" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="360" decoding="async" height="34" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg/42px-Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg/63px-Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg/84px-Ambox_current_red_Asia_Australia.svg.png 2x" width="42"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section needs to be <b>updated</b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help update this article to reflect recent events or newly available information.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2022</span>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The rate of cancer during pregnancy is 0.02–1%, and in many cases, cancer of the mother leads to consideration of abortion to protect the life of the mother, or in response to the potential damage that may occur to the fetus during treatment. This is particularly true for <a href="/wiki/Cervical_cancer" title="Cervical cancer">cervical cancer</a>, the most common type of which occurs in 1 of every 2,000–13,000 pregnancies, for which initiation of treatment "cannot co-exist with preservation of fetal life (unless <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoadjuvant_chemotherapy" title="Neoadjuvant chemotherapy">neoadjuvant chemotherapy</a> is chosen)". Very early stage cervical cancers (I and IIa) may be treated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radical_hysterectomy" title="Radical hysterectomy">radical hysterectomy</a> and pelvic <a href="/wiki/Lymph_node" title="Lymph node">lymph node</a> dissection, <a href="/wiki/Radiation_therapy" title="Radiation therapy">radiation therapy</a>, or both, while later stages are treated by radiotherapy. Chemotherapy may be used simultaneously. Treatment of breast cancer during pregnancy also involves fetal considerations, because <a href="/wiki/Lumpectomy" title="Lumpectomy">lumpectomy</a> is discouraged in favor of modified <a href="/wiki/Radical_mastectomy" title="Radical mastectomy">radical mastectomy</a> unless late-term pregnancy allows follow-up radiation therapy to be administered after the birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weisz_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weisz-172">[171]</a></sup>
</p><p>Exposure to a single chemotherapy drug is estimated to cause a 7.5–17% risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teratogenic" title="Teratogenic">teratogenic</a> effects on the fetus, with higher risks for multiple drug treatments. Treatment with more than 40 <a href="/wiki/Gray_(unit)" title="Gray (unit)">Gy</a> of radiation usually causes spontaneous abortion. Exposure to much lower doses during the first trimester, especially 8 to 15 weeks of development, can cause <a href="/wiki/Intellectual_disability" title="Intellectual disability">intellectual disability</a> or <a href="/wiki/Microcephaly" title="Microcephaly">microcephaly</a>, and exposure at this or subsequent stages can cause reduced intrauterine growth and birth weight. Exposures above 0.005–0.025 Gy cause a dose-dependent reduction in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IQ" title="IQ">IQ</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weisz_172-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weisz-172">[171]</a></sup> It is possible to greatly reduce exposure to radiation with abdominal shielding, depending on how far the area to be irradiated is from the fetus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11237773_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11237773-174">[173]</a></sup>
</p><p>The process of birth itself may also put the mother at risk. According to Li <i>et al.</i>, "[v]aginal delivery may result in dissemination of neoplastic cells into lymphovascular channels, haemorrhage, cervical laceration and implantation of malignant cells in the episiotomy site, while abdominal delivery may delay the initiation of non-surgical treatment."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19197101_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19197101-175">[174]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_and_religion">History and religion</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_abortion" title="History of abortion">History of abortion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Religion_and_abortion" title="Religion and abortion">Religion and abortion</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AngkorWatAbortionAD1150.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="656" data-file-width="952" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/AngkorWatAbortionAD1150.JPG/220px-AngkorWatAbortionAD1150.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/AngkorWatAbortionAD1150.JPG/330px-AngkorWatAbortionAD1150.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/AngkorWatAbortionAD1150.JPG/440px-AngkorWatAbortionAD1150.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AngkorWatAbortionAD1150.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bas-relief" title="Bas-relief">Bas-relief</a> at <a href="/wiki/Angkor_Wat" title="Angkor Wat">Angkor Wat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, c. 1150, depicting a <a href="/wiki/Demon" title="Demon">demon</a> inducing an abortion by pounding the abdomen of a pregnant woman with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pestle" title="Pestle">pestle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potts_90-2"><a href="#cite_note-potts-90">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Since <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_times" title="Ancient times">ancient times</a>, abortions have been done using a number of methods, including <a href="/wiki/Herbal_medicine" title="Herbal medicine">herbal medicines</a> acting as <a href="/wiki/Abortifacient" title="Abortifacient">abortifacients</a>, sharp tools through the use of <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">force</a>, or through other <a href="/wiki/Traditional_medicine" title="Traditional medicine">traditional medicine</a> methods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1-31">[30]</a></sup> Induced abortion has a long history and can be traced back to civilizations as varied as ancient China (abortifacient knowledge is often attributed to the mythological ruler <a href="/wiki/Shennong" title="Shennong">Shennong</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_India" title="Ancient India">ancient India</a> since its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vedic_age" title="Vedic age">Vedic age</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Misra2006_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Misra2006-178">[177]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> with its <a href="/wiki/Ebers_Papyrus" title="Ebers Papyrus">Ebers Papyrus</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1550 BCE</span>), and the Roman Empire in the time of <a href="/wiki/Juvenal" title="Juvenal">Juvenal</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 200 CE</span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1-31">[30]</a></sup> One of the earliest known artistic representations of abortion is in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bas_relief" title="Bas relief">bas relief</a> at Angkor Wat (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 1150</span>). Found in a series of <a href="/wiki/Frieze" title="Frieze">friezes</a> that represent judgment after death in <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hindu</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> culture, it depicts the technique of abdominal abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-potts_90-3"><a href="#cite_note-potts-90">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Judaism_and_abortion" title="Judaism and abortion">In Judaism</a> (<span class="nourlexpansion nowrap"><a class="external text" href="https://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Genesis+2%3A7&amp;version=NRSV" rel="nofollow">Genesis 2:7</a></span>), the fetus is not considered to have a human soul until it is safely outside of the woman, is viable, and has taken its first breath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup> The fetus is considered valuable property of the woman and not a human life while in the womb (<a class="external text" href="https://mechon-mamre.org/p/pt/pt0221.htm#22" rel="nofollow">Exodus 21:22–23</a>). While <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> encourages people to <a href="/wiki/Be_fruitful_and_multiply" title="Be fruitful and multiply">be fruitful and multiply</a> by having children, abortion is allowed and is deemed necessary when a pregnant woman's life is in danger.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup> Several religions, including Judaism, which disagree that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_life_begins_at_conception" title="Human life begins at conception">human life begins at conception</a>, support the legality of abortion on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_freedom" title="Religious freedom">religious freedom</a> grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georgian_2022_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Georgian_2022-184">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some medical scholars and abortion opponents have suggested that the <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Oath" title="Hippocratic Oath">Hippocratic Oath</a> forbade physicians in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a> from performing abortions;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1-31">[30]</a></sup> other scholars disagree with this interpretation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1-31">[30]</a></sup> and state that the medical texts of <a href="/wiki/Hippocratic_Corpus" title="Hippocratic Corpus">Hippocratic Corpus</a> contain descriptions of abortive techniques right alongside the Oath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup> The physician <a href="/wiki/Scribonius_Largus" title="Scribonius Largus">Scribonius Largus</a> wrote in 43 CE that the Hippocratic Oath prohibits <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Surgery_in_Ancient_Rome" title="Surgery in Ancient Rome">abortion</a>, as did <a href="/wiki/Soranus_of_Ephesus" title="Soranus of Ephesus">Soranus of Ephesus</a>, although apparently not all doctors adhered to it strictly at the time. According to Soranus' 1st or 2nd century CE work <i>Gynaecology</i>, one party of medical practitioners banished all abortives as required by the Hippocratic Oath; the other party to which he belonged was willing to prescribe abortions only for the sake of the mother's health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Soranus_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Soranus-186">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Largus_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Largus-187">[186]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/Politics_(Aristotle)" title="Politics (Aristotle)">Politics</a></i> (350 BCE), <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> condemned infanticide as a means of population control. He preferred abortion in such cases,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[188]</a></sup> with the restriction that it "must be practised on it before it has developed sensation and life; for the line between lawful and unlawful abortion will be marked by the fact of having sensation and being alive."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190"><a href="#cite_note-190">[189]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_and_abortion" title="Catholic Church and abortion">Catholic Church</a>, opinion was divided on how serious abortion was in comparison with such acts as contraception, oral sex, and sex in marriage for pleasure rather than procreation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noonan_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noonan-191">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 155–167">: 155–167 </span></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> did not begin vigorously opposing abortion until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1_31-5"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georgian_2022_184-1"><a href="#cite_note-Georgian_2022-184">[183]</a></sup> As early as ~100 A.D., the <i><a href="/wiki/Didache" title="Didache">Didache</a></i> taught that abortion was sinful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[191]</a></sup> Several historians argue that prior to the 19th century most Catholic authors did not regard termination of pregnancy before <a href="/wiki/Quickening" title="Quickening">quickening</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ensoulment" title="Ensoulment">ensoulment</a> as an abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup> Among these authors were the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctors_of_the_Church" title="Doctors of the Church">Doctors of the Church</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Augustine" title="St. Augustine">St. Augustine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Thomas_Aquinas" title="St. Thomas Aquinas">St. Thomas Aquinas</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Alphonsus_Liguori" title="St. Alphonsus Liguori">St. Alphonsus Liguori</a>. <a href="/wiki/Pope_Sixtus_V" title="Pope Sixtus V">Pope Sixtus V</a> (<span style="white-space:nowrap;"><abbr title="reigned">r.</abbr> </span> 1585–90) was the only Pope before <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_IX" title="Pope Pius IX">Pope Pius IX</a> (in his 1869 bull, <i>Apostolicae Sedis</i>) to institute a Church policy labeling all abortion as homicide and condemning abortion regardless of the stage of pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noonan_191-1"><a href="#cite_note-Noonan-191">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 362–364">: 362–364 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-riddle2_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-riddle2-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 157–158">: 157–158 </span></sup> Sixtus V 1588 pronouncement was reversed in 1591 by <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_XIV" title="Pope Gregory XIV">Pope Gregory XIV</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup> In the recodification of <a href="/wiki/1917_Code_of_Canon_Law" title="1917 Code of Canon Law">1917 Code of Canon Law</a>, <i>Apostolicae Sedis</i> was strengthened, in part to remove a possible reading that excluded excommunication of the mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup> Statements made in the <i><a href="/wiki/Catechism_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Catechism of the Catholic Church">Catechism of the Catholic Church</a></i>, the codified summary of the Church's teachings, considers abortion from the moment of conception as homicide and called for the end of legal abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2014 Guttmacher survey of abortion patients in the United States found that many reported a religious affiliation: 24% were Catholic while 30% were Protestant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200"><a href="#cite_note-200">[199]</a></sup> A 1995 survey reported that Catholic women are as likely as the general population to terminate a pregnancy, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protestants" title="Protestants">Protestants</a> are less likely to do so, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Christians" title="Evangelical Christians">evangelical Christians</a> are the least likely to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guttmacher_7-5"><a href="#cite_note-guttmacher-7">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bankole98_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-bankole98-8">[7]</a></sup> A 2019 <a href="/wiki/Pew_Research_Center" title="Pew Research Center">Pew Research Center</a> study found that most <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denominations</a> were against overturning <i><a href="/wiki/Roe_v._Wade" title="Roe v. Wade">Roe v. Wade</a>,</i> which in the United States legalized abortion, at around 70%, except White Evangelicals at 35%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PewReseach2019_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-PewReseach2019-201">[200]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Islam_and_abortion" title="Islam and abortion">In Islam</a>, abortion is traditionally permitted until a point in time when Muslims believe the soul enters the fetus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1_31-6"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1-31">[30]</a></sup> considered by various theologians to be at conception, 40 days after conception, 120 days after conception, or quickening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC_and_Islam_/_Abortion_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_and_Islam_/_Abortion-202">[201]</a></sup> Abortion is largely heavily restricted or forbidden in areas of high <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> faith such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_East_and_North_Africa" title="Middle East and North Africa">Middle East and North Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[202]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FrenchPeriodicalPills-January61845,BostonDailyTimes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="776" decoding="async" height="290" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/FrenchPeriodicalPills-January61845%2CBostonDailyTimes.jpg/220px-FrenchPeriodicalPills-January61845%2CBostonDailyTimes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/FrenchPeriodicalPills-January61845%2CBostonDailyTimes.jpg/330px-FrenchPeriodicalPills-January61845%2CBostonDailyTimes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/FrenchPeriodicalPills-January61845%2CBostonDailyTimes.jpg/440px-FrenchPeriodicalPills-January61845%2CBostonDailyTimes.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FrenchPeriodicalPills-January61845,BostonDailyTimes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"French Periodical Pills" was an example of a clandestine advertisement published in a January 1845 edition of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boston_Daily_Times" title="Boston Daily Times">Boston Daily Times</a></i>.</div></div></div>
<p>Abortion has been a fairly common practice,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup> and was not always illegal or controversial until the 19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardin_1978_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hardin_1978-206">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207"><a href="#cite_note-207">[206]</a></sup> Under <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a>, including early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_common_law" title="English common law">English common law</a> dating back to <a href="/wiki/Edward_Coke" title="Edward Coke">Edward Coke</a> in 1648,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alford_2003_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alford_2003-208">[207]</a></sup> abortion was generally permitted before quickening (14–26 weeks after conception, or betweeth the fourth and sixth month),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup> and at women's discretion;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georgian_2022_184-2"><a href="#cite_note-Georgian_2022-184">[183]</a></sup> it was whether abortion was performed after quickening that determined if it was a crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alford_2003_208-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alford_2003-208">[207]</a></sup> In Europe and North America, abortion techniques advanced starting in the 17th century; the <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservatism</a> of most in the medical profession with regards to sexual matters prevented the wide expansion of abortion techniques.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1_31-7"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[211]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup> Other medical practitioners in addition to some physicians advertised their services, and they were not widely regulated until the 19th century when the practice, sometimes called <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Restellism" title="Restellism">restellism</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup> was banned in both the United States and the United Kingdom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1_31-8"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[nb 2]</a></sup>
</p><p>Church groups, as well as physicians, were highly influential in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-abortion_movement" title="Anti-abortion movement">anti-abortion movements</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1_31-9"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardin_1978_206-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hardin_1978-206">[205]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_2022_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-NPR_2022-216">[214]</a></sup> and religious groups more so since the 20th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samuels_&amp;_Potts_2022_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Samuels_&amp;_Potts_2022-217">[215]</a></sup> Some of the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-abortion_laws" title="Anti-abortion laws">anti-abortion laws</a> punished only the doctor or abortionist,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georgian_2022_184-4"><a href="#cite_note-Georgian_2022-184">[183]</a></sup> and while women could be criminally tried for a <a href="/wiki/Self-induced_abortion" title="Self-induced abortion">self-induced abortion</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alford_2003_208-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alford_2003-208">[207]</a></sup> they were rarely prosecuted in general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardin_1978_206-2"><a href="#cite_note-Hardin_1978-206">[205]</a></sup> In the United States, some argued that abortion was more dangerous than childbirth until about 1930 when incremental improvements in abortion procedures relative to childbirth made abortion safer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[nb 3]</a></sup> Others maintain that in the 19th century early abortions under the hygienic conditions in which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midwives" title="Midwives">midwives</a> usually worked were relatively safe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219"><a href="#cite_note-219">[216]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220"><a href="#cite_note-220">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221"><a href="#cite_note-221">[218]</a></sup> Several scholars argue that, despite improved medical procedures, the period from the 1930s until the 1970s saw more zealous enforcement of anti-abortion laws, and concomitantly an increasing control of abortion providers by organized crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222"><a href="#cite_note-222">[nb 4]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_Russia" title="Soviet Russia">Soviet Russia</a> (1919), <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a> (1935), and <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a> (1938) were among the first countries to legalize certain or all forms of abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cbctrust_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-cbctrust-223">[219]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> (1935), a law permitted abortions for those deemed "hereditarily ill", while women considered of German stock were specifically prohibited from having abortions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224"><a href="#cite_note-224">[220]</a></sup> Preceding the Nazi German example was that of some 19th-century physicians, one of the most famous and consequential being <a href="/wiki/Horatio_Storer" title="Horatio Storer">Horatio Storer</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samuels_&amp;_Potts_2022_217-1"><a href="#cite_note-Samuels_&amp;_Potts_2022-217">[215]</a></sup> who argued for anti-abortion laws on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racist" title="Racist">racist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pseudoscientific" title="Pseudoscientific">pseudoscientific</a> grounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NPR_2022_216-1"><a href="#cite_note-NPR_2022-216">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225"><a href="#cite_note-225">[221]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[222]</a></sup> Beginning in the second half of the 20th century, abortion was legalized in a greater number of countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1_31-10"><a href="#cite_note-Management_of_Abortion,_Chp_1-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Societal_attitudes_towards_abortion" title="Societal attitudes towards abortion">Societal attitudes towards abortion</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Abortion_debate">Abortion debate</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abortion_debate" title="Abortion debate">Abortion debate</a></div>
<p>Induced abortion has long been the source of considerable debate. <a href="/wiki/Medical_ethics" title="Medical ethics">Ethical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">moral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_aspects_of_the_abortion_debate" title="Philosophical aspects of the abortion debate">philosophical</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Therapeutic_abortion" title="Therapeutic abortion">biological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethics_in_religion" title="Ethics in religion">religious</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abortion_law" title="Abortion law">legal</a> issues surrounding abortion are related to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Value_system" title="Value system">value systems</a>. Opinions of abortion may be about <a href="/wiki/Fetal_rights" title="Fetal rights">fetal rights</a>, governmental authority, and <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a>.
</p><p>In both public and private debate, arguments presented in favor of or against abortion access focus on either the moral permissibility of an induced abortion, or justification of laws permitting or restricting abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227"><a href="#cite_note-227">[223]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Medical_Association" title="World Medical Association">World Medical Association</a> Declaration on Therapeutic Abortion notes, "circumstances bringing the interests of a mother into conflict with the interests of her unborn child create a dilemma and raise the question as to whether or not the pregnancy should be deliberately terminated."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[224]</a></sup> Abortion debates, especially pertaining to <a href="/wiki/Abortion_law" title="Abortion law">abortion laws</a>, are often spearheaded by groups advocating one of these two positions. Groups who favor greater legal restrictions on abortion, including complete prohibition, most often describe themselves as "pro-life" while groups who are against such legal restrictions describe themselves as "<a href="/wiki/Abortion-rights_movements" title="Abortion-rights movements">pro-choice</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[225]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_abortion_law">Modern abortion law</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Abortion_law" title="Abortion law">Abortion law</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_abortion_law_debate" title="History of abortion law debate">History of abortion law debate</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abortion_Laws.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1399" data-file-width="2756" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Abortion_Laws.svg/400px-Abortion_Laws.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Abortion_Laws.svg/600px-Abortion_Laws.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Abortion_Laws.svg/800px-Abortion_Laws.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abortion_Laws.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><b><a href="/wiki/Abortion_law" title="Abortion law">Legality of abortion by country or territory</a></b></div> <table> <tbody><tr> <td colspan="2">Legal on request: </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#40D0FF; color:black;"> </span></td> <td>No <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gestational_age_(obstetrics)" title="Gestational age (obstetrics)">gestational limit</a> </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#4080FF; color:black;"> </span></td> <td>Gestational limit after the first 17 weeks </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#3465A4; color:white;"> </span></td> <td>Gestational limit in the first 17 weeks </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#A000FF; color:white;"> </span></td> <td>Unclear gestational limit </td></tr> <tr> <td colspan="2">Legally restricted to cases of: </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#73D216; color:black;"> </span></td> <td><a href="/wiki/Maternal_death" title="Maternal death">Risk to woman's life</a>, <a href="/wiki/Complications_of_pregnancy" title="Complications of pregnancy">to her health</a>*, <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">rape</a>*, <a href="/wiki/Birth_defect" title="Birth defect">fetal impairment</a>*, or <a href="/wiki/Socioeconomic_status" title="Socioeconomic status">socioeconomic factors</a> </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#EDD400; color:black;"> </span></td> <td>Risk to woman's life, to her health*, rape, or fetal impairment </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#F540F5; color:black;"> </span></td> <td>Risk to woman's life, to her health*, or fetal impairment </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#C17D11; color:black;"> </span></td> <td>Risk to woman's life*, to her health*, or rape </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#F57900; color:black;"> </span></td> <td>Risk to woman's life or to her health </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#CC0000; color:white;"> </span></td> <td>Risk to woman's life </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#000000; color:white;"> </span></td> <td>Illegal with no exceptions </td></tr> <tr valign="top"> <td><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="legend-color" style="background-color:#B9B9B9; color:black;"> </span></td> <td>No information </td></tr> <tr> <td colspan="2">* Does not apply to some countries or territories in that category </td></tr></tbody></table> <b>Note</b>: In some countries or territories, abortion laws are modified by other laws, <a href="/wiki/Regulation" title="Regulation">regulations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Legal_doctrine" title="Legal doctrine">legal principles</a> or <a href="/wiki/Judicial_opinion" title="Judicial opinion">judicial decisions</a>. This map shows their combined effect as implemented by the authorities.</div></div></div>
<p>Current laws pertaining to abortion are diverse. Religious, moral, and cultural factors continue to influence abortion laws throughout the world. The <a href="/wiki/Right_to_life" title="Right to life">right to life</a>, the right to liberty, the right to <a href="/wiki/Security_of_person" title="Security of person">security of person</a>, and the right to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reproductive_health" title="Reproductive health">reproductive health</a> are major issues of human rights that sometimes constitute the basis for the existence or absence of abortion laws.
</p><p>In jurisdictions where abortion is legal, certain requirements must often be met before a woman may obtain a legal abortion (an abortion performed without the woman's consent is considered <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Feticide" title="Feticide">feticide</a>). These requirements usually depend on the age of the fetus, often using a <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy#Terminology" title="Pregnancy">trimester</a>-based system to regulate the window of legality, or as in the U.S., on a doctor's evaluation of the fetus' <a href="/wiki/Fetal_viability" title="Fetal viability">viability</a>. Some jurisdictions require a waiting period before the procedure, prescribe the distribution of information on <a href="/wiki/Prenatal_development" title="Prenatal development">fetal development</a>, or require that <a href="/wiki/Minors_and_abortion" title="Minors and abortion">parents be contacted</a> if their minor daughter requests an abortion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[226]</a></sup> Other jurisdictions may require that a woman obtain the <a href="/wiki/Paternal_rights_and_abortion" title="Paternal rights and abortion">consent of the fetus' father</a> before aborting the fetus, that abortion providers inform women of health risks of the procedure—sometimes including "risks" not supported by the medical literature—and that multiple medical authorities certify that the abortion is either medically or socially necessary. Many restrictions are waived in emergency situations. China, which has ended their<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[227]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/One-child_policy" title="One-child policy">one-child policy</a>, and now has a two child policy,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[228]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-233"><a href="#cite_note-233">[229]</a></sup> has at times incorporated mandatory abortions as part of their population control strategy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-234"><a href="#cite_note-234">[230]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other jurisdictions ban abortion almost entirely. Many, but not all, of these allow legal abortions in a variety of circumstances. These circumstances vary based on jurisdiction, but may include whether the pregnancy is a result of rape or incest, the fetus' development is impaired, the woman's physical or mental well-being is endangered, or socioeconomic considerations make childbirth a hardship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dev98-07_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Dev98-07-32">[31]</a></sup> In countries where abortion is banned entirely, such as <a href="/wiki/Abortion_in_Nicaragua" title="Abortion in Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a>, medical authorities have recorded rises in maternal death directly and indirectly due to pregnancy as well as deaths due to doctors' fears of prosecution if they treat other gynecological emergencies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[231]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[232]</a></sup> Some countries, such as Bangladesh, that nominally ban abortion, may also support clinics that perform abortions under the guise of menstrual hygiene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[233]</a></sup> This is also a terminology in traditional medicine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nations1977_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-nations1977-238">[234]</a></sup> In places where abortion is illegal or carries heavy social stigma, pregnant women may engage in <a href="/wiki/Medical_tourism" title="Medical tourism">medical tourism</a> and travel to countries where they can terminate their pregnancies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[235]</a></sup> Women without the means to travel can resort to providers of illegal abortions or attempt to perform an abortion by themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[236]</a></sup>
</p><p>The organization <a href="/wiki/Women_on_Waves" title="Women on Waves">Women on Waves</a> has been providing education about medical abortions since 1999. The NGO created a mobile medical clinic inside a shipping container, which then travels on rented ships to countries with restrictive abortion laws. Because the ships are registered in the Netherlands, Dutch law prevails when the ship is in international waters. While in port, the organization provides free workshops and education; while in international waters, medical personnel are legally able to prescribe medical abortion drugs and counseling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-242"><a href="#cite_note-242">[238]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[239]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sex-selective_abortion">Sex-selective abortion</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sex-selective_abortion" title="Sex-selective abortion">Sex-selective abortion</a></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medical_ultrasonography" title="Medical ultrasonography">Sonography</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amniocentesis" title="Amniocentesis">amniocentesis</a> allow parents to determine sex before childbirth. The development of this technology has led to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sex-selective_abortion_and_female_infanticide" title="Sex-selective abortion and female infanticide">sex-selective abortion</a>, or the termination of a fetus based on its sex. The selective termination of a female fetus is most common.
</p><p>Sex-selective abortion is partially responsible for the noticeable disparities between the birth rates of male and female children in some countries. The preference for male children is reported in many areas of Asia, and abortion used to limit female births has been reported in Taiwan, South Korea, India, and China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[240]</a></sup> This deviation from the standard birth rates of males and females occurs despite the fact that the country in question may have officially banned sex-selective abortion or even sex-screening.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[241]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-246"><a href="#cite_note-246">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOC_India_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-LOC_India-247">[243]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[244]</a></sup> In China, a historical preference for a male child has been exacerbated by the <a href="/wiki/One-child_policy" title="One-child policy">one-child policy</a>, which was enacted in 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[245]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many countries have taken legislative steps to reduce the incidence of sex-selective abortion. At the <a href="/wiki/International_Conference_on_Population_and_Development" title="International Conference on Population and Development">International Conference on Population and Development</a> in 1994 over 180 states agreed to eliminate "all forms of discrimination against the girl child and the root causes of son preference",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNFPA_250-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNFPA-250">[246]</a></sup> conditions also condemned by a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Assembly_of_the_Council_of_Europe" title="Parliamentary Assembly of the Council of Europe">PACE</a> resolution in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[247]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> and <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a>, along with other United Nations agencies, have found that measures to reduce access to abortion are much less effective at reducing sex-selective abortions than measures to reduce gender inequality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNFPA_250-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNFPA-250">[246]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-abortion_violence">Anti-abortion violence</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anti-abortion_violence" title="Anti-abortion violence">Anti-abortion violence</a></div>
<p>In a number of cases, abortion providers and these facilities have been subjected to various forms of violence, including murder, attempted murder, kidnapping, stalking, assault, arson, and bombing. Anti-abortion violence is classified by both governmental and scholarly sources as terrorism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-csis_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-csis-252">[248]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-253"><a href="#cite_note-253">[249]</a></sup> In the U.S. and Canada, over 8,000 incidents of violence, trespassing, and death threats have been recorded by providers since 1977, including over 200 bombings/arsons and hundreds of assaults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[250]</a></sup> The majority of abortion opponents have not been involved in violent acts.
</p><p>In the United States, four physicians who performed abortions have been murdered: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/David_Gunn_(doctor)" title="David Gunn (doctor)">David Gunn</a> (1993), <a href="/wiki/John_Britton_(doctor)" title="John Britton (doctor)">John Britton</a> (1994), <a href="/wiki/Barnett_Slepian" title="Barnett Slepian">Barnett Slepian</a> (1998), and <a href="/wiki/George_Tiller" title="George Tiller">George Tiller</a> (2009). Also murdered, in the U.S. and Australia, have been other personnel at abortion clinics, including receptionists and security guards such as James Barrett, Shannon Lowney, Lee Ann Nichols, and Robert Sanderson. Woundings (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Garson_Romalis" title="Garson Romalis">Garson Romalis</a>) and attempted murders have also taken place in the United States and Canada. Hundreds of bombings, arsons, acid attacks, invasions, and incidents of vandalism against abortion providers have occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-255"><a href="#cite_note-255">[251]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naf_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-naf-256">[252]</a></sup> Notable perpetrators of anti-abortion violence include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eric_Robert_Rudolph" title="Eric Robert Rudolph">Eric Robert Rudolph</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scott_Roeder" title="Scott Roeder">Scott Roeder</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shelley_Shannon" title="Shelley Shannon">Shelley Shannon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Jennings_Hill" title="Paul Jennings Hill">Paul Jennings Hill</a>, the first person to be executed in the United States for murdering an abortion provider.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[253]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Legal_protection_of_access_to_abortion" title="Legal protection of access to abortion">Legal protection of access to abortion</a> has been brought into some countries where abortion is legal. These laws typically seek to protect abortion clinics from obstruction, vandalism, picketing, and other actions, or to protect women and employees of such facilities from threats and harassment.
</p><p>Far more common than physical violence is psychological pressure. In 2003, Chris Danze organized anti-abortion organizations throughout Texas to prevent the construction of a <a href="/wiki/Planned_Parenthood" title="Planned Parenthood">Planned Parenthood</a> facility in Austin. The organizations <a href="/wiki/Doxing" title="Doxing">released the personal information</a> online of those involved with construction, sent them up to 1200 phone calls a day and contacted their churches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoan20072_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoan20072-258">[254]</a></sup> Some protestors record women entering clinics on camera.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDoan20072_258-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDoan20072-258">[254]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-human_examples"><span class="anchor" id="Other_animals"></span>Non-human examples</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">Miscarriage</a></div>
<p>Spontaneous abortion occurs in various animals. For example, in sheep it may be caused by stress or physical exertion, such as crowding through doors or being chased by dogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-259"><a href="#cite_note-259">[255]</a></sup> In cows, abortion may be caused by contagious disease, such as <a href="/wiki/Brucellosis" title="Brucellosis">brucellosis</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Campylobacter" title="Campylobacter">Campylobacter</a></i>, but can often be controlled by vaccination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-260"><a href="#cite_note-260">[256]</a></sup> Eating <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pine_needle" title="Pine needle">pine needles</a> can also induce abortions in cows.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-261"><a href="#cite_note-261">[257]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-262"><a href="#cite_note-262">[258]</a></sup>
Several plants, including <a href="/wiki/Gutierrezia_sarothrae" title="Gutierrezia sarothrae">broomweed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Veratrum_californicum" title="Veratrum californicum">skunk cabbage</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conium_maculatum" title="Conium maculatum">poison hemlock</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nicotiana_glauca" title="Nicotiana glauca">tree tobacco</a>, are known to cause fetal deformities and abortion in cattle<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirkbride_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kirkbride-263">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 45–46">: 45–46 </span></sup> and in sheep and goats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kirkbride_263-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kirkbride-263">[259]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 77–80">: 77–80 </span></sup> In horses, a fetus may be aborted or resorbed if it has <a href="/wiki/Lethal_white_syndrome" title="Lethal white syndrome">lethal white syndrome</a> (congenital intestinal aganglionosis). Foal embryos that are homozygous for the <a href="/wiki/Dominant_white" title="Dominant white">dominant white</a> gene (WW) are theorized to also be aborted or resorbed before birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-phj_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-phj-264">[260]</a></sup> In many species of sharks and rays, stress-induced abortions occur frequently on capture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[261]</a></sup>
</p><p>Viral infection can cause abortion in dogs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dogabort1_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-dogabort1-266">[262]</a></sup> Cats can experience spontaneous abortion for many reasons, including hormonal imbalance. A combined abortion and spaying is performed on pregnant cats, especially in <a href="/wiki/Trap%E2%80%93neuter%E2%80%93return" title="Trap–neuter–return">trap–neuter–return</a> programs, to prevent unwanted kittens from being born.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spay1_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-spay1-267">[263]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spay2_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-spay2-268">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spay3_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-spay3-269">[265]</a></sup>
Female rodents may terminate a pregnancy when exposed to the smell of a male not responsible for the pregnancy, known as the <a href="/wiki/Bruce_effect" title="Bruce effect">Bruce effect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwagmeyer_270-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwagmeyer-270">[266]</a></sup>
</p><p>Abortion may also be induced in animals, in the context of <a href="/wiki/Animal_husbandry" title="Animal husbandry">animal husbandry</a>. For example, abortion may be induced in mares that have been mated improperly, or that have been purchased by owners who did not realize the mares were pregnant, or that are pregnant with twin foals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[267]</a></sup> Feticide can occur in horses and zebras due to male harassment of pregnant mares or forced copulation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[268]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[269]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[270]</a></sup> although the frequency in the wild has been questioned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-275"><a href="#cite_note-275">[271]</a></sup> Male <a href="/wiki/Gray_langur" title="Gray langur">gray langur</a> monkeys may attack females following male takeover, causing miscarriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[272]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a list of definitions as stated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obstetrics_and_gynecology" title="Obstetrics and gynecology">obstetrics and gynecology</a> (OB/GYN) textbooks, dictionaries, and other sources, see <i><a href="/wiki/Definitions_of_abortion" title="Definitions of abortion">Definitions of abortion</a></i>. Definitions of abortion vary from source to source, and language used to define abortion often reflects societal and political opinions, not only scientific knowledge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-215"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-215">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the United States, the first laws related to abortion beginning in the 1820s were made to protect women from real or perceived risks, and those more restrictive penalized only the provider. By 1859, abortion was not a crime in 21 out of 33 states, and was prohibited only post-quickening, while penalties for pre-quickening abortions were lower. This changed starting in the 1860s under the influence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-immigrant" title="Anti-immigrant">anti-immigrant</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anti-Catholic" title="Anti-Catholic">anti-Catholic</a> sentiment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Georgian_2022_184-3"><a href="#cite_note-Georgian_2022-184">[183]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-218"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-218">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">By 1930, medical procedures in the United States had improved for both childbirth and abortion but not equally, and induced abortion in the first trimester had become safer than childbirth. In 1973, <i>Roe v. Wade</i> acknowledged that abortion in the first trimester was safer than childbirth. For sources, see:

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1011085734" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width" style="column-width: 30em;">

<li id="cite_note-Ray2014-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Ray2014_11-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ray2014_11-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ray2014_11-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFRaymondGrossmanWeaverToti2014">Raymond EG, Grossman D, Weaver MA, Toti S, Winikoff B (November 2014). "Mortality of induced abortion, other outpatient surgical procedures and common activities in the United States". <i>Contraception</i>. <b>90</b> (5): 476–479. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.contraception.2014.07.012" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.contraception.2014.07.012</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/25152259" rel="nofollow">25152259</a>. <q>Results: The abortion-related mortality rate in 2000-2009 in the United States was 0.7 per 100,000 abortions. Studies in approximately the same years found mortality rates of 0.8-1.7 deaths per 100,000 plastic surgery procedures, 0-1.7 deaths per 100,000 dental procedures, 0.6-1.2 deaths per 100,000 marathons run and at least 4 deaths among 100,000 cyclists in a large annual bicycling event. The traffic fatality rate per 758 vehicle miles traveled by passenger cars in the United States in 2007-2011 was about equal to the abortion-related mortality rate. Conclusions: The safety of induced abortion as practiced in the United States for the past decade met or exceeded expectations for outpatient surgical procedures and compared favorably to that of two common nonmedical voluntary activities.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Contraception&amp;rft.atitle=Mortality+of+induced+abortion%2C+other+outpatient+surgical+procedures+and+common+activities+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.volume=90&amp;rft.issue=5&amp;rft.pages=476-479&amp;rft.date=2014-11&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.contraception.2014.07.012&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F25152259&amp;rft.aulast=Raymond&amp;rft.aufirst=EG&amp;rft.au=Grossman%2C+D&amp;rft.au=Weaver%2C+MA&amp;rft.au=Toti%2C+S&amp;rft.au=Winikoff%2C+B&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbortion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Grimes2012-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Grimes2012_12-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Grimes2012_12-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFRaymondGrimes2012">Raymond EG, Grimes DA (February 2012). "The comparative safety of legal induced abortion and childbirth in the United States". <i>Obstetrics and Gynecology</i>. <b>119</b> (2 Pt 1): 215–219. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1097%2FAOG.0b013e31823fe923" rel="nofollow">10.1097/AOG.0b013e31823fe923</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/22270271" rel="nofollow">22270271</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:25534071" rel="nofollow">25534071</a>. <q>Conclusion: Legal induced abortion is markedly safer than childbirth. The risk of death associated with childbirth is approximately 14 times higher than that with abortion. Similarly, the overall morbidity associated with childbirth exceeds that with abortion.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Obstetrics+and+Gynecology&amp;rft.atitle=The+comparative+safety+of+legal+induced+abortion+and+childbirth+in+the+United+States&amp;rft.volume=119&amp;rft.issue=2+Pt+1&amp;rft.pages=215-219&amp;rft.date=2012-02&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A25534071%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F22270271&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1097%2FAOG.0b013e31823fe923&amp;rft.aulast=Raymond&amp;rft.aufirst=EG&amp;rft.au=Grimes%2C+DA&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbortion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-WHO-preventing-unsafe-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-WHO-preventing-unsafe_13-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.who.int/news-room/fact-sheets/detail/preventing-unsafe-abortion" rel="nofollow">"Preventing unsafe abortion"</a>. World Health Organization. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20190823190843/https://www.who.int/news-room/fact-sheets/detail/preventing-unsafe-abortion" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 23 August 2019<span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFFaúndesShah2015">Faúndes A, Shah IH (October 2015). <a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.ijgo.2015.03.018" rel="nofollow">"Evidence supporting broader access to safe legal abortion"</a>. <i>International Journal of Gynaecology and Obstetrics</i>. World Report on Women's Health 2015: The unfinished agenda of women's reproductive health. <b>131</b> (Suppl 1): S56–S59. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.ijgo.2015.03.018" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.ijgo.2015.03.018</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/26433508" rel="nofollow">26433508</a>. <q>A strong body of accumulated evidence shows that the simple means to drastically reduce unsafe abortion-related maternal deaths and morbidity is to make abortion legal and institutional termination of pregnancy broadly accessible. ... criminalization of abortion only increases mortality and morbidity without decreasing the incidence of induced abortion, and that decriminalization rapidly reduces abortion-related mortality and does not increase abortion rates.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=International+Journal+of+Gynaecology+and+Obstetrics&amp;rft.atitle=Evidence+supporting+broader+access+to+safe+legal+abortion&amp;rft.volume=131&amp;rft.issue=Suppl+1&amp;rft.pages=S56-S59&amp;rft.date=2015-10&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.ijgo.2015.03.018&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F26433508&amp;rft.aulast=Fa%C3%BAndes&amp;rft.aufirst=A&amp;rft.au=Shah%2C+IH&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fdoi.org%2F10.1016%252Fj.ijgo.2015.03.018&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbortion"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Sedgh_2012-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Sedgh_2012_26-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Sedgh_2012_26-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Sedgh_2012_26-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Sedgh_2012_26-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Sedgh_2012_26-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Sedgh_2012_26-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Sedgh_2012_26-6"><sup><i><b>g</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Sedgh_2012_26-7"><sup><i><b>h</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFSedghSinghShahAhman2012">Sedgh G, Singh S, Shah IH, Ahman E, Henshaw SK, Bankole A (February 2012). <a class="external text" href="http://www.guttmacher.org/pubs/journals/Sedgh-Lancet-2012-01.pdf" rel="nofollow">"Induced abortion: incidence and trends worldwide from 1995 to 2008"</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span>. <i>Lancet</i>. <b>379</b> (9816): 625–632. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2FS0140-6736%2811%2961786-8" rel="nofollow">10.1016/S0140-6736(11)61786-8</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/22264435" rel="nofollow">22264435</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:27378192" rel="nofollow">27378192</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120206043854/http://www.guttmacher.org/pubs/journals/Sedgh-Lancet-2012-01.pdf" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> <span class="cs1-format">(PDF)</span> from the original on 6 February 2012. <q>Because few of the abortion estimates were based on studies of random samples of women, and because we did not use a model-based approach to estimate abortion incidence, it was not possible to compute confidence intervals based on standard errors around the estimates. Drawing on the information available on the accuracy and precision of abortion estimates that were used to develop the subregional, regional, and worldwide rates, we computed intervals of certainty around these rates (webappendix). We computed wider intervals for unsafe abortion rates than for safe abortion rates. The basis for these intervals included published and unpublished assessments of abortion reporting in countries with liberal laws, recently published studies of national unsafe abortion, and high and low estimates of the numbers of unsafe abortion developed by WHO.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Lancet&amp;rft.atitle=Induced+abortion%3A+incidence+and+trends+worldwide+from+1995+to+2008&amp;rft.volume=379&amp;rft.issue=9816&amp;rft.pages=625-632&amp;rft.date=2012-02&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A27378192%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F22264435&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2FS0140-6736%2811%2961786-8&amp;rft.aulast=Sedgh&amp;rft.aufirst=G&amp;rft.au=Singh%2C+S&amp;rft.au=Shah%2C+IH&amp;rft.au=Ahman%2C+E&amp;rft.au=Henshaw%2C+SK&amp;rft.au=Bankole%2C+A&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.guttmacher.org%2Fpubs%2Fjournals%2FSedgh-Lancet-2012-01.pdf&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbortion"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-Gutt_2018_fact-28"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Gutt_2018_fact_28-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="https://www.guttmacher.org/fact-sheet/induced-abortion-worldwide" rel="nofollow">"Induced Abortion Worldwide"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Guttmacher_Institute" title="Guttmacher Institute">Guttmacher Institute</a></i>. 1 March 2018. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200223022612/https://www.guttmacher.org/fact-sheet/induced-abortion-worldwide" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 23 February 2020<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">21 February</span> 2020</span>. <q>Of the world's 1.64 billion women of reproductive age, 6% live where abortion is banned outright, and 37% live where it is allowed without restriction as to reason. Most women live in countries with laws that fall between these two extremes.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Guttmacher+Institute&amp;rft.atitle=Induced+Abortion+Worldwide&amp;rft.date=2018-03-01&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.guttmacher.org%2Ffact-sheet%2Finduced-abortion-worldwide&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbortion"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-eos-elective-46"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-eos-elective_46-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.surgeryencyclopedia.com/Ce-Fi/Elective-Surgery.html#b" rel="nofollow">"Elective surgery"</a>. "An elective surgery is a planned, non-emergency surgical procedure. It may be either medically required (e.g., cataract surgery), or optional (e.g., breast augmentation or implant) surgery.</span>

<li id="cite_note-Fjerstad_2009b-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Fjerstad_2009b_66-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFFjerstadSivinLichtenbergTrussell2009">Fjerstad M, Sivin I, Lichtenberg ES, Trussell J, Cleland K, Cullins V (September 2009). <a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3766037" rel="nofollow">"Effectiveness of medical abortion with mifepristone and buccal misoprostol through 59 gestational days"</a>. <i>Contraception</i>. <b>80</b> (3): 282–286. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.contraception.2009.03.010" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.contraception.2009.03.010</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMC_(identifier)" title="PMC (identifier)">PMC</a> <span class="cs1-lock-free" title="Freely accessible"><a class="external text" href="//www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/pmc/articles/PMC3766037" rel="nofollow">3766037</a></span>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PMID_(identifier)" title="PMID (identifier)">PMID</a> <a class="external text" href="//pubmed.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/19698822" rel="nofollow">19698822</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Contraception&amp;rft.atitle=Effectiveness+of+medical+abortion+with+mifepristone+and+buccal+misoprostol+through+59+gestational+days&amp;rft.volume=80&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=282-286&amp;rft.date=2009-09&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3766037%23id-name%3DPMC&amp;rft_id=info%3Apmid%2F19698822&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.contraception.2009.03.010&amp;rft.aulast=Fjerstad&amp;rft.aufirst=M&amp;rft.au=Sivin%2C+I&amp;rft.au=Lichtenberg%2C+ES&amp;rft.au=Trussell%2C+J&amp;rft.au=Cleland%2C+K&amp;rft.au=Cullins%2C+V&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov%2Fpmc%2Farticles%2FPMC3766037&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAbortion"></span> The regimen (200 mg of mifepristone, followed 24–48 hours later by 800 mcg of <i>vaginal</i> misoprostol) <i>previously</i> used by <a href="/wiki/Planned_Parenthood" title="Planned Parenthood">Planned Parenthood</a> clinics in the United States from 2001 to March 2006 was 98.5% effective through 63 days gestation—with an ongoing pregnancy rate of about 0.5%, and an additional 1% of women having uterine evacuation for various reasons, including problematic bleeding, persistent gestational sac, clinician judgment or a woman's request. The regimen (200 mg of mifepristone, followed 24–48 hours later by 800 mcg of <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/buccal" title="wikt:buccal">buccal</a></i> misoprostol) <i>currently</i> used by Planned Parenthood clinics in the United States since April 2006 is 98% effective through 59 days gestation.</span>
</li>

GLOWM.10444. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.3843%2FGLOWM.10444" rel="nofollow">10.3843/GLOWM.10444</a>. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20150924082507/http://www.glowm.com/section_view/heading/Labor%20Induction%20Termination%20of%20Pregnancy/item/443" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 24 September 2015<span class="reference-accessdate">.
